Causes of Differing Narrations from a Hanafi Imam
Listing causes for conflicting narrations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes of differing narrations from a Hanafi imam and apply the Hanafi principle for resolving such differences in fatwa.

Core Idea
Conflicting narrations from one imam often stem from methodological tools or transmission error, not genuine contradiction.

When studying Hanafi fiqh, you will encounter differing rulings attributed to the same imam, especially Abu Hanifa. This passage from a classical work on rasm al-mufti explains the reasons behind such differences. Understanding these causes is essential for a mufti to correctly weigh narrations and issue sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
List the four causes
Identify each cause mentioned in the passage: error in hearing, retraction, qiyas vs istihsan, and hukm vs ihtiyat.
2
Analyze the author's critique
The author argues that the first two causes (error and retraction) are less plausible when a single transmitter like Imam Muhammad narrates both versions. Consider why this weakens those explanations.
The author prefers to restrict to the latter two causes in many cases.
3
Understand the role of qiyas and istihsan
If an imam gives one answer by qiyas and another by istihsan, both are valid in different contexts. Recognize that each method has its own evidentiary basis.
In Hanafi usul, istihsan can override qiyas when stronger evidence exists.
4
Recognize hukm vs ihtiyat
The imam may give a ruling based on the actual legal rule (hukm) and a stricter one based on precaution (ihtiyat). Understand that the mufti should choose the appropriate one.
The ihtiyat answer is often used when uncertainty exists.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given two narrations on a sale contract, one based on qiyas and one on istihsan, determine which is more likely relied upon. State the principle of preferring istihsan in cases of need or custom.
Later Hanafi authorities often give preference to the narration based on istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف القول والرواية
منها: الغلط في السماع، كأن يجيب بحرف النفي إذا سئل عن حادثة، ويقول : لا يجوز ، فيشتبه على الراوي فينقل ما سمع. ومنها: أن يكون له قول قد رجع عنه ويعلم بعض من يختلف إليه رجوعه فيروي الثاني والآخر لم يعلمه فيروي الأول.
ومنها: أن يكون قال أحدهما على وجه القياس، والآخر على وجه الاستحسان، فيسمع كل واحد أحدهما، فينقل كما سمع". ومنها: أن يكون الجواب في مسألة من وجهين: من جهة الحكم، ومن جهة الاحتياط، فينقل كل كما سمع. قلت: فعلى ما عدا الوجه الأول يكون الاختلاف في الروايتين من جهة المنقول عنه أيضا لابتناء الاختلاف فيهما على اختلاف القولين المرويين، فيكونان من باب واحد ويؤيده أن ناقل الروايتين قد يكون ،واحدا، فإن إحدى الروايتين قد تكون في كتاب من كتب الأصول، والأخرى في كتب النوادر ، بل قد يكون كل منهما في كتب الأصول، والكل من جَمْع واحد وهو الإمام محمد حله، وهذا ينافي الوجه الأول، ويبعد الوجه الثاني، فالأظهر الاقتصار على الوجهين الأخيرين، لكن لا في كل فرع اختلفت فيه الرواية، بل بعض ذلك قد يكون لأحدهما، والبعض الآخر للآخر. لكن هذا إنما يتأتى فيما يصلح أن يكون فيه قياس واستحسان أو احتياط وغيره، نعم! يتأتى الوجهان الأولان فيما إذا اختلف الراوي.
وقد يقال: إن من وجوه الاختلاف أيضا، تردد المجتهد في الحكم لتعارض الأدلة عنده بلا ،مرجح أو لاختلاف رأيه في مدلول الدليل الواحد، فإن الدليل قد يكون محتملا لوجهين أو أكثر، فيبني على كل واحد جوابا، ثم قد يترجح عنده أحدهما، فينسب إليه، ولهذا تراهم يقولون: "قال أبو حنيفة كذا وفي رواية عنه كذا"، وقد لا يترجح عنده أحدهما فيستوي رأيه فيهما، ولذا تراهم يحكون عنه في مسألة القولين على وجه يفيد تساويهما عنده فيقولون: "وفي المسألة عنه روايتان أو قولان".
Difference of statement and narration: Among them: error in hearing, such as when he answers with a negative particle when asked about an incident, and says: 'It is not permissible,' so the narrator confuses it and transmits what he heard. Among them: that he has a statement from which he has retracted, and some of those who frequent him know his retraction, so he narrates the second, while another does not know it and narrates the first. Among them: that one of them [the two answers] was given by way of qiyas, and the other by way of istihsan, so each one hears one of them and transmits what he heard. Among them: that the answer in a matter is from two aspects: from the aspect of ruling (hukm) and from the aspect of precaution (ihtiyat), so each transmits what he heard. I say: So apart from the first aspect, the difference in the two narrations is also due to the one from whom it is transmitted, because the difference in them is based on the difference of the two transmitted statements, so they are of the same category. This is supported by the fact that the transmitter of the two narrations may be one, for one of the two narrations may be in a book of the usul books, and the other in the books of nawadir; rather, each of them may be in the usul books, and all from a single compiler, Imam Muhammad (may he be pleased with him). This contradicts the first aspect and makes the second aspect unlikely. So the apparent view is to restrict to the latter two aspects, but not in every branch where narration differs; rather, some of that may be due to the first, and some due to the other. But this only applies to what can involve qiyas and istihsan or ihtiyat and other than that. Yes! The first two aspects apply when the narrator differs. It may be said: Among the aspects of difference also is the mujtahid's hesitation in the ruling due to conflicting evidence before him without a preferable weight, or due to his differing opinion on the indication of a single evidence. For the evidence may be susceptible to two or more aspects, so he builds an answer on each one, then one of them may become preponderant in his view, so it is attributed to him. For this reason you see them saying: 'Abu Hanifa said such-and-such, and in a narration from him such-and-such.' And one of them may not become preponderant in his view, so his opinion is equal regarding them. For this reason you see them narrating from him in a matter two statements in a manner that indicates their equality in his view, so they say: 'In the matter there are two narrations or two statements from him.'
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage discusses the causes for differing narrations (ikhtilaf al-qaul wa al-riwaya) from a single Hanafi imam, particularly Abu Hanifa. The author lists four reasons: (1) error in hearing by the narrator, such as mishearing 'la yajuz' as 'yajuz'; (2) the imam retracting a previous statement, with some transmitters knowing the retraction and others not; (3) one answer being based on qiyas (analogy) and another on istihsan (juristic preference), each transmitter reporting only what they heard; (4) the answer having two aspects—one based on the actual ruling (hukm) and the other on precaution (ihtiyat). The author then comments that except for the first reason, the difference stems from the imam himself having multiple statements. He notes that even a single transmitter like Imam Muhammad may narrate both in different books (al-Usul and al-Nawadir), negating the idea of a hearing error. Therefore, the preferred view is to limit causes to the latter two (qiyas/istihsan and hukm/ihtiyat), but not universally—some cases truly involve hearing errors or retractions. The passage also adds that hesitation due to conflicting evidence without a preponderant factor can lead to two equal opinions, thus attributed as two narrations.

Key Terms
الغلط في السماع (al-ghalat fi al-sama'): A mistake made by the transmitter in hearing the answer, leading to a misreported ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive a ruling for new cases based on an existing ruling from the primary sources.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference, a method where a ruling is chosen over qiyas due to stronger evidence, custom, or necessity.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyat): Precaution in issuing a fatwa, giving the stricter or safer answer when there is uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a cause for differing narrations that the author considers less likely when a single transmitter reports both versions?
A) The imam used qiyas in one and istihsan in another.
B) The imam gave one answer based on hukm and another based on ihtiyat.
C) The imam retracted a previous statement.
D) The imam hesitated due to conflicting evidence.

Key Takeaway
Differing narrations from a Hanafi imam typically arise from methodological differences (qiyas vs istihsan) or context (hukm vs ihtiyat), not from inconsistency, and a mufti must apply the school's tarjih principles.

Attributing Two Equal Opinions to a Mujtahid
Ruling on attributing two conflicting opinions to a single mujtahid when they are equal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three scholarly positions on attributing two equally weighted opinions to a mujtahid and apply the Hanafi rule that both are attributed.

Core Idea
When evidence is truly balanced, both opinions are the mujtahid's; neither is rejected.

In the practice of ifta', a mufti must often rely on the preponderant (raajih) opinion of his school. But what happens when a mujtahid himself considers two opinions equally strong because the evidence is contradictory? This lesson examines a rule from Imam al-Qarafi: when preponderance is impossible, the mujtahid may choose either, and both opinions remain correctly attributed to him. We will explore the reasoning behind this and contrast it with alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
Normally, a mujtahid or muqallid must give the preponderant (raajih) opinion. A fatwa by other than the raajih is impermissible unless the circumstances allow.
This is the baseline from which the exception operates.
2
Identify the exception
When evidence is conflicting and the mujtahid is unable to preponderate (tarjih), he may judge by whichever he wishes because the opinions are equal in his view. This is the trigger for the attribution rule.
The condition is genuine tasawi (equality), not mere uncertainty.
3
Apply the attribution rule
Because both opinions are equally valid in his eyes, it is correct to attribute both to him. This refutes the views that attribute none or only one.
The author explicitly rejects the idea that one must be considered retracted.
4
Distinguish further scenarios
If one opinion becomes preponderant without the mujtahid abandoning the other, then the preponderant is attributed and the other is mentioned as a narration. If he abandons it entirely, only the preponderant remains his statement. However, the earlier disagreement does not vanish; later scholars may still note it.
The passage ends by noting that retraction does not erase the historical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسبة القولين إلى مجتهد]
وقد قدمنا عن الإمام القرافي أنه لا يحل الحكم والإفتاء بغير الراجح لمجتهد أو مقلد إلا إذا تعارضت الأدلة عند المجتهد، وعجز عن الترجيح. أي فإنّ له الحكم بأيهما شاء؛ لتساويهما عنده. وعلى هذا فيصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه لا كما يقوله بعض الأصوليين من أنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما، وما يقوله بعضهم من اعتقاد نسبة أحدهما إليه؛ لأن رجوعه عن الآخر غير معين؛ إذ الفرض تساويهما في رأيه، وعدم ترجح أحدهما على الآخر. نعم إذا ترجح عنده أحدهما مع عدم إعراضه عن الآخر عنه، ينسب إليه الراجح عنده ويذكر الثاني رواية عنه، أما لو أعرض الآخر بالكلية، لم يبق قولا له بل يكون قوله هو الراجح فقط، لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع، كما قاله بعض الشافعية، وأيده بعضهم بأن أهل عصر إذا أجمعوا على قول بعد اختلافهم، فقد حكى الأصوليون قولين في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى.
عن
ورجوعه
Attributing two statements to a mujtahid: We have already presented from Imam al-Qarafi that it is not permissible to judge or give fatwa by other than the preponderant (raajih) for a mujtahid or a muqallid, unless the evidence is conflicting before the mujtahid and he is unable to preponderate. That is, he may judge by whichever he wishes, because they are equal in his view. On this basis, it is correct to attribute both statements to him, not as some usulis say that nothing is attributed to him from them, nor as some say that only one is attributed to him because his retraction from the other is not specified. For the assumption is that they are equal in his view, and neither preponderates over the other. Yes, if one becomes preponderant in his view without him turning away from the other, then the preponderant is attributed to him, and the second is mentioned as a narration from him. But if he turns away from the other completely, it is no longer his statement; rather, his statement is only the preponderant. However, the difference in the matter does not disappear after his retraction, as some Shafi'is said, and some supported it by saying that if the people of an age reach consensus on a statement after their difference, the usulis have mentioned two opinions about the removal of the previous disagreement; so what is not subject to consensus is more apt.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses the attribution of two conflicting opinions to a mujtahid when they are equal in his view due to conflicting evidence. It cites Imam al-Qarafi's rule that a mujtahid (or muqallid) cannot issue a fatwa with a non-preponderant opinion unless he is unable to preponderate; then he can choose either. Therefore, both opinions are correctly attributed to him. This contradicts two other views: (1) that none are attributed, and (2) that only one is attributed because the other is considered retracted without specification. The author argues that since the mujtahid holds both as equally valid, both are his. However, if one becomes preponderant but he has not abandoned the other, the preponderant is attributed and the other is a narration. If he abandons it entirely, only the preponderant remains his statement, but the earlier disagreement does not simply vanish; the matter still has differing positions among scholars.

Key Terms
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A scholar qualified to derive rulings directly from the Quran and Sunnah through ijtihad.
الراجح (al-raajih): The preponderant opinion that is stronger or more likely correct based on evidence or reasoning.
التساوي (al-tasawi): Equality of two opinions in the mujtahid's view, with no preponderance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a mujtahid holds two opinions as equally valid due to conflicting evidence, which is correct?
A) Both opinions are attributed to him.
B) Only one opinion is attributed, and the other is considered abandoned.
C) Neither opinion is attributed to him.
D) He must choose one and discard the other.

Key Takeaway
When a mujtahid is unable to preponderate between two equally supported opinions, both correctly count as his positions and may be followed.

The Ruling on Fatwa When Evidences Conflict Equally
Explaining the rule on fatwa when evidences conflict equally and attribution of opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when evidences conflict equally without preponderance, the mujtahid must seek (tahari) and act on one, and that only that opinion is attributed to him, while the other is a narration if not abandoned.

Core Idea
When evidences conflict equally, act on one by tahari; only that opinion is yours, the other is a riwaya.

In the practice of fatwa, a mufti must base his rulings on the preponderant evidence. But what if two pieces of evidence appear equally strong? Imam al-Qarafi's rule, presented here, clarifies that the mujtahid may choose either after tahari, and both are validly attributed to him—contrary to the view that none is attributed. This lesson unpacks the conditions for attributing opinions and the distinction between a preponderant opinion and a transmitted narration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
State the general principle: a mujtahid or muqallid must only give fatwa with the preponderant (rajih) evidence.
This is the baseline.
2
Recognize the exception for equal evidences
When evidences conflict and the mujtahid cannot preponderate one over the other, he may rule with either of his choosing by tahari.
Tahari is seeking the stronger through conscience.
3
Attribute opinions correctly
If the mujtahid chooses one, both opinions are validly attributed to him—unlike the claim that nothing is attributed. If one becomes preponderant later without abandoning the other, the preponderant is his opinion and the other is a riwaya. If he fully abandons one, it ceases to be his opinion.
Abandonment removes it from fatwa, but the dispute remains unless consensus.
4
Note the Shafi'i nuance
Some Shafi'is argue that the earlier disagreement is not removed by later adoption of one opinion, unless there is consensus.
This touches on the binding effect of consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسبة القولين إلى مجتهد]
وقد قدمنا عن الإمام القرافي أنه لا يحل الحكم والإفتاء بغير الراجح لمجتهد أو مقلد إلا إذا تعارضت الأدلة عند المجتهد، وعجز عن الترجيح. أي فإنّ له الحكم بأيهما شاء؛ لتساويهما عنده. وعلى هذا فيصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه لا كما يقوله بعض الأصوليين من أنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما، وما يقوله بعضهم من اعتقاد نسبة أحدهما إليه؛ لأن رجوعه عن الآخر غير معين؛ إذ الفرض تساويهما في رأيه، وعدم ترجح أحدهما على الآخر. نعم إذا ترجح عنده أحدهما مع عدم إعراضه عن الآخر عنه، ينسب إليه الراجح عنده ويذكر الثاني رواية عنه، أما لو أعرض الآخر بالكلية، لم يبق قولا له بل يكون قوله هو الراجح فقط، لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع، كما قاله بعض الشافعية، وأيده بعضهم بأن أهل عصر إذا أجمعوا على قول بعد اختلافهم، فقد حكى الأصوليون قولين في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى.
إمكان القولين لمجتهد]
لكن ما ذكر في كتب الأصول عندنا من أنه لا يمكن أن يكون للمجتهد قولان كما مر - ينافي ذلك؛ لأنه مبني – فيما يظهر – على ما ذكروا في تعارض الأدلة أنه إذا وقع التعارض بين آيتين يصار إلى الحديث، فإن تعارض فإلى أقوال الصحابة فإن تعارضت فإلى القياس، فإن تعارض قياسان ولا ترجيح فإنه يتحرى فيهما، ويعمل بشهادة قلبه، فإذا عمل بأحدهما، ليس له العمل بالآخر إلا بدليل فوق التحري. قالوا: وقال الشافعي يعمل بأيهما شاء من غير تحر. ولهذا صار له في المسألة قولان وأكثر. وأما الروايتان عن أصحابنا في مسألة واحدة، فإنما كانتا في وقتين، فإحداهما صحيحة دون الأخرى، لكن لم تعرف المتأخرة منهما. وعلى هذا فما يقال: "فيه عن الإمام روايتان، فلعدم معرفة الأخير، وما يقال فيه: "وفي رواية عنه كذا إما لعلمهم بأنها قوله الأول، أو لكون هذه الرواية رويت عنه في غير كتب الأصول، وهذا أقرب. لكن لا يخفى أن ما ذكروه في بحث تعارض الأدلة مشكل؛ لأنه يلزم منه أن يكون ما فيه روايتان عن الإمام لا يجوز فيه العمل بواحدة منهما؛ لعدم العلم بالصحيحة من الباطلة منهما، وأنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما، كما مر عن بعض الأصوليين، عن بعض الأصوليين، مع أن ذلك واقع في مسائل لا تحصى، ونراهم يرجحون إحدى الروايتين على الأخرى، وينسبونها إليه. فالذي يظهر ما مر عن الإمام البليغي من بيان تعدد
Attribution of two opinions to a mujtahid. We have previously mentioned from Imam al-Qarafi that it is not permissible to rule or give fatwa with anything other than the preponderant (rajih) for a mujtahid or a muqallid, except when evidences conflict for the mujtahid and he is unable to make a tarjih. That is, he may rule with whichever of them he wishes because they are equal in his view. Based on this, it is valid to attribute each of the two opinions to him, not as some usulis say that nothing is attributed to him, and as some say that one of them is attributed to him because his abandonment of the other is unspecified; since the assumption is that they are equal in his view and neither outweighs the other. Yes, if one outweighs the other in his view while he has not turned away from the other, the preponderant is attributed to him and the second is mentioned as a narration from him. But if he completely turns away from the other, it is no longer an opinion of his; rather his opinion is only the preponderant. However, the disagreement in the issue is not removed after returning, as some Shafi'is said, and some supported it by saying that if the people of an era agree on an opinion after their disagreement, the usulis have related two opinions regarding the removal of the previous disagreement. So if there is no consensus, it is more so. [New heading] Possibility of two opinions for a mujtahid. But what is mentioned in our books of usul that it is not possible for a mujtahid to have two opinions, as passed, contradicts that because it is based—apparently—on what they mentioned regarding conflicting evidences: if conflict occurs between two verses, one turns to the hadith; if conflict, then to the sayings of the companions; if conflict, then to qiyas; if two qiyas conflict and there is no tarjih, then he makes tahari (search) and acts according to the testimony of his heart. If he acts by one of them, it is not permissible for him to act by the other except with evidence above tahari. They said: al-Shafi'i said he may act by whichever he wishes without tahari. For this reason, he has two or more opinions on an issue. As for the two narrations from our scholars on one issue, they were only from two different times, so one is correct and the other is not, but the later one is not known. Based on this, what is said: 'There are two narrations from the Imam' is due to not knowing the latter; and what is said: 'And in a narration from him such and such' is either because they know it is his first opinion, or because this narration was narrated from him in non-usul books, and this is more likely. But it is not hidden that what they mentioned in the discussion of conflicting evidences is problematic because it necessarily implies that what has two narrations from the Imam cannot be acted upon by either of them due to not knowing which is correct and which is incorrect, and that nothing is attributed to him, as passed from some usulis, even though such cases are countless and we see them giving preponderance to one of the two narrations over the other and attributing them to him. So what appears is what passed from Imam al-Bulighi about the clarification of multiplicity.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an extract from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti discussing the attribution of two opinions to a mujtahid and the possibility of a mujtahid having two opinions. The author begins by stating the rule from Imam al-Qarafi: a mujtahid or muqallid must only give fatwa with the preponderant opinion unless the evidences are contradictory and tarjih is impossible; then the mujtahid may choose either, and both opinions are validly attributed to him. This refutes the view of some usulis that no opinion is attributed to the mujtahid in such a case. If one opinion becomes preponderant while the other is not abandoned, the preponderant is attributed and the other is called a 'riwaya'. If the mujtahid completely abandons the other opinion, it is no longer his opinion. However, the disagreement in the issue remains unless there is consensus, as some Shafi'is noted. The second part addresses a potential contradiction: Hanafi usul books state that a mujtahid cannot have two opinions simultaneously because when evidences conflict, a Hanafi performs tahari (seeking) and acts on one, not on the other. In contrast, al-Shafi'i allows acting on either without tahari, thus he can have multiple opinions. The Hanafi two narrations from a single imam are actually from different times, so only the later is correct, but if the later is unknown, both are mentioned as riwayat. The author notes the practical difficulty: Hanafi scholars still weigh and attribute such narrations, contradicting the strict usul rule. The preferred explanation is from Imam al-Bulighi about multiplicity.

Key Terms
التحري (al-tahari): The process of seeking the stronger evidence through personal conscience when evidences are equally strong.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Giving preponderance to one evidence over another based on strength.
الراجح (al-rajih): The preponderant opinion that is stronger according to the mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, when a mujtahid finds two equally strong evidences and cannot preponderate, what is the rule regarding attributing opinions to him?
A) No opinion is attributed to him because the evidences are equal.
B) He may act on one by tahari, and both opinions are validly attributed to him.
C) Only one opinion is attributed, the other is invalid.
D) He must ask for a third opinion from another scholar.

Key Takeaway
When evidences conflict equally, a Hanafi mufti performs tahari and acts on one; both opinions are validly attributed to him, with the other becoming a riwaya if not abandoned.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
Can a Mujtahid Have Two Opinions? The Hanafi Position
Explaining that Hanafi imams cannot have two opinions simultaneously, contrasting with Shafi'i, and addressing the reality of two narrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school denies a mujtahid having two simultaneous opinions, distinguish between Hanafi narrations (from different times) and Shafi'i multiple opinions, and evaluate the practical problem of attributing two narrations to one imam.

Core Idea
Hanafi imams have only one opinion per issue at a time; two narrations reflect different times, not simultaneous choice.

A common confusion in fiqh is attributing two contradictory opinions to the same imam. The Hanafi usul position, as discussed in the second part of our passage, firmly states that a mujtahid cannot have two opinions on one issue simultaneously. This lesson explores the reasoning behind that position, contrasts it with the Shafi'i view, and addresses the practical challenge that despite this principle, Hanafi works often list two narrations from an imam. The resolution lies in understanding that these narrations come from different periods of the imam's life.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi usul principle
Hanafi usul holds that a mujtahid cannot have two opinions on an issue at the same time because when evidences conflict, he must perform tahari and act on one; the other is not his opinion.
This is based on the method of resolving conflicting evidences.
2
Contrast with Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i allows acting on either conflicting evidence without tahari, leading to the possibility of multiple opinions on the same issue.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the number of attributed opinions.
3
Explain Hanafi narrations
Two narrations from a Hanafi imam on the same issue are from different times. Only the later one is correct, but if unknown, both are transmitted as riwayat.
Thus, they are not simultaneous opinions.
4
Analyze the practical problem
Despite this principle, Hanafi scholars regularly weigh two narrations and attribute them to the imam. This creates an apparent contradiction, which the author tries to resolve by citing al-Bulighi's explanation of multiplicity.
The passage ends abruptly, suggesting further elaboration.

According to the Hanafi explanation in the passage, why do two narrations from a Hanafi imam exist on the same issue?
A) Because the imam changed his view over time, and only the later is correct.
B) Because both narrations are equally valid, reflecting the imam's own doubt.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mujtahid cannot have two simultaneous opinions; two narrations represent different times and only the later is correct, though the later may be unknown, leading to practical tarjih.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Statements of Abu Hanifa's Disciples Are His Statements in Reality
Explaining that disciples' statements are attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the status of the disciples' statements according to Hanafi rasm al-mufti and apply this principle to understand the unity of the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
The statements of Abu Hanifa's major disciples are legally attributed to him, because they are based on his principles and authorization.

This passage establishes a foundational principle in Hanafi jurisprudential hierarchy: the opinions of the great disciples are not independent but an extension of the Imam's own ijtihad. Understanding this helps clarify why apparent divergences within the school are not true disagreements but variations within a unified method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The author states that the disciples' statements are literally the Imam's statements. Find where this is asserted.
2
Understand the basis: the Imam's authorization
Abu Hanifa told his companions to follow evidence if it appears to them. This shows they were authorized to derive rulings using his principles.
3
Examine supporting evidence from disciples
Abu Yusuf and Zufar testify that any difference was based on something the Imam had said and later retracted. This reinforces that their rulings are not novel.
4
Analyze the conclusion from al-Hawi al-Qudsi
Acting on any disciple's statement is acting on the Imam's statement. The disciples swore that all their statements are narrations from Abu Hanifa.
5
Apply the rule
Recognize that in Hanafi fatwa hierarchy, the opinions of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and Hasan are authoritative and are treated as the Imam's own.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقوال أصحاب الإمام أقواله حقيقةً
الأوجه في اختلاف الرواية عن الإمام مع زيادة ما ذكرناه من تردده في الحكمين، واحتمال كل منهما في رأيه مع عدم مرجح عنده لأحدهما من دليل أو تحر، أو غيره. فتأمل.
ثم لا يخفى أن هذا الوجه الذي قلناه أكثر اطرادا من الأوجه الأربعة المارة في اختلاف الروايتين؛ لشموله ما فيه استحسان، أو احتياط، وغيره. [أقوال أصحاب الإمام أقواله حقيقةً]
11
إذا تقرر ذلك فاعلم أن الإمام أبا حنيفة محلليه [؟] من شدة احتياطه وورعه وعلمه بأن الاختلاف من آثار الرحمة - قال لأصحابه: "إن توجه لكم دليل فقولوا به فكان كل يأخذ برواية عنه ويرجحها، كما حكاه في "الدر المختار". وفي "الولوالجية" من كتاب الجنايات قال أبو يوسف: "ما قلت قولاً خالفت فيه أبا حنيفة إلا قولاً قد كان قاله وروي عن زفر أنه قال: "ما خالفت أبا حنيفة في شيء إلا قد قاله ثم رجع عنه". فهذا إشارة إلى أنهم ما سلكوا طريق الخلاف بل قالوا ما قالوا عن اجتهاد ورأي؛ اتباعا لما قاله أستاذهم أبو حنيفة. وفي آخر "الحاوي القدسي : وإذا أخذ بقول واحد منهم، يعلم قطعا أنه يكون به آخذاً بقول أبي حنيفة؛ فإنه روي عن جميع أصحابه الكبار كأبي يوسف ومحمد ،وزفر، والحسن، أنهم قالوا: "ما قلنا في مسألة قولاً إلا وهو روايتنا عن أبي حنيفة". وأقسموا عليه أيمانا غلاظا، فلم يتحقق إذن في الفقه جواب ولا مذهب إلا له كيف ما كان، وما نسب إلى غيره إلا بطريق المجاز للموافقة.
The statements of the Imam's disciples are his statements in reality. The most frequent aspect in the variation of narration from the Imam, with the addition of what we mentioned of his hesitation in two rulings and the possibility of each in his opinion without having a preference for one over the other from a proof or investigation or otherwise. Reflect. Then it is not hidden that this aspect which we have mentioned is more consistent than the four aspects mentioned in the variation of two narrations, because it includes what contains istihsan or precaution or other matters. [The statements of the Imam's disciples are his statements in reality] Once this is established, know that Imam Abu Hanifa, his companions [?] from the intensity of his caution, piety, and knowledge that difference is from the effects of mercy, said to his companions: 'If a proof appears to you, then speak according to it.' So each would take a narration from him and give it preponderance, as narrated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. In al-Walwaljiyya from the Book of Criminal Law, Abu Yusuf said: 'I never said a statement in which I differed from Abu Hanifa except a statement that he had already said.' And it is narrated from Zufar that he said: 'I never differed from Abu Hanifa in anything except that he had said it and then retracted from it.' This indicates that they did not take the path of disagreement but rather spoke from ijtihad and opinion, following what their teacher Abu Hanifa said. At the end of al-Hawi al-Qudsi: 'And if one acts on the statement of any one of them, he certainly knows that by doing so he is acting on the statement of Abu Hanifa. For it is narrated from all his major companions, such as Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and Hasan, that they said: 'We never said a statement on any issue except that it is our narration from Abu Hanifa.' And they swore strong oaths upon it. Therefore, no answer or madhhab is established in fiqh except his, however it may be, and what is attributed to others is only metaphorically due to agreement.'
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule establishing the status of the statements of Abu Hanifa's disciples. The author begins by explaining that the most common form of variation in narrations from the Imam involves his hesitation between two rulings without a clear preference. This aspect is more consistent than other types of divergence. He then recounts that Abu Hanifa, out of extreme caution and piety, told his companions to follow any evidence that appears to them. Consequently, each disciple would choose a narration from the Imam and strengthen it. Reports from Abu Yusuf and Zufar confirm that any apparent disagreement was actually based on something the Imam had earlier said and later retracted. The author concludes that the disciples did not engage in independent disagreement; their statements are ultimately an application of the Imam's own ijtihad. The passage from al-Hawi al-Qudsi further emphasizes that acting on a disciple's statement is tantamount to acting on the Imam's statement, as the disciples swore that every ruling they issued was a narration from Abu Hanifa. Hence, in Hanafi fiqh, all rulings and the madhhab itself are attributed to Abu Hanifa, either literally or metaphorically.

Key Terms
أقوال أصحاب الإمام (aqwāl aṣḥāb al-imām): The statements of the Imam's disciples (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Hasan) which are considered his statements in reality due to his authorization and their reliance on his principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because Imam authorized them to follow evidence derived from his principles.
C) Because they never differed from him.
D) Because they only narrated from him.

Key Takeaway
The opinions of Abu Hanifa's major disciples are legally considered his statements, ensuring the unity of the Hanafi madhhab.

The Principle ‘If a Hadith is Sahih, it is My Madhhab’: Scope and Conditions
Addressing objection and establishing a rule for acting on sahih hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle 'إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي' and identify the condition that qualifies a mufti to act on a sahih hadith contrary to the known Hanafi position.

Core Idea
A sahih hadith contrary to the Hanafi position is still part of the madhhab, but only for those qualified to evaluate evidence.

This passage addresses an important objection: if an imam retracts a ruling, how can his disciples' dissenting statements still be attributed to him? The author resolves this by noting that the Imam authorized his disciples to use his principles, making their rulings extensions of his. He then draws an analogy to the famous principle narrated from Abu Hanifa: 'If a hadith is sahih, it is my madhhab.' This lesson explains that principle, its textual basis, and the crucial qualification that it applies only to those with the capacity to examine evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The passage begins with a hypothetical objection: when a mujtahid retracts a statement, it no longer remains his. So how can statements of his disciples that contradict him be attributed to him? Understand why this is problematic for the Hanafi school's attribution of rulings.
This objection challenges the internal consistency of the madhhab.
2
Analyze the author's resolution
The author answers by saying that the Imam ordered his companions to take what they find evidence for based on his principles. Therefore, their rulings are built on his foundations and are not completely abandoned; they are his statements from one aspect.
This resolution preserves the unity of the madhhab while allowing for ijtihad within its framework.
3
Connect to the sahih hadith principle
The author then presents an analogy: the principle 'If a sahih hadith is found, it is my madhhab' (narrated from Abu Hanifa). This means that when a qualified scholar finds a sahih hadith contrary to the known position, acting on it is considered following the Imam's true madhhab, not leaving it.
This principle is widely transmitted and accepted across schools.
4
Understand the condition of qualification
The author clarifies that this principle applies only to one who is 'ahl al-nazar' — capable of examining texts, distinguishing clear from abrogated, and evaluating evidence. It is not a blanket permission for any muqallid.
The condition ensures that the principle is not misused.
5
Recognize the source and authority
The rule is supported by transmission from Abu Hanifa himself via Ibn 'Abd al-Bar and others. It is also cited in works like Ibn al-Shahnah's commentary and al-Sha'rani's works, indicating its authority in the tradition.
The rule is not a later invention but originates from the Imam.

Source Text

If you say: When a mujtahid retracts a statement, it no longer remains his statement, because it becomes like an abrogated ruling, as will come. Consequently, what his disciples said in disagreement with him is not his madhhab; rather their statements become their own madhhabs, so how can it be attributed to him? And the Hanafi only follows Abu Hanifa, so it is attributed to him and not others. I say: I used to find this problematic, and I answered it in my gloss on Radd al-Muhtar 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar: Because the Imam ordered his companions to take from his statements what they find evidence for, so what they said became his statement, since it is based on the principles he established for them; thus it was not retracted from every aspect. [Acting on hadith if it is sahih] An analogy to this is what 'Allama al-Bayri narrated in the beginning of his commentary on al-Ashbah from Sharh al-Hidayah of Ibn al-Shahnah al-Kabir, father of the commentator of al-Wahbaniyya and teacher of Ibn al-Humam, its text: 'If a hadith is sahih and conflicts with the madhhab, act on the hadith, and that becomes his madhhab, and his muqallid does not exit from being Hanafi by acting on it. For it is authentically reported from Abu Hanifa that he said: 'If a hadith is sahih, it is my madhhab.' This was narrated by Imam Ibn 'Abd al-Bar from Abu Hanifa and other imams.' Imam al-Sha'rani also narrated it from the four imams. I say: It is not hidden that this applies to one who is qualified to examine texts and know their clear from abrogated. So when the people of the madhhab look at the evidence and act on it, it is attributed to the madhhab because it is issued with the permission of the founder of the madhhab; there is no doubt that if he knew of the weakness of his evidence, he would have retracted and followed the stronger evidence. Commentary
This passage addresses an objection and then presents a key rule for muftis. The objection: if an imam retracts a ruling, it is no longer his, so how can his disciples' dissenting statements still be attributed to him? The author resolves this by noting that the Imam authorized his disciples to follow evidence derived from his principles, so their statements are effectively his—abandoned from one angle but not from another. He then draws an analogy to the famous principle reported from Abu Hanifa: 'If a sahih hadith is found, it is my madhhab.' This means that when a qualified scholar encounters a sahih hadith contrary to the established Hanafi ruling, acting on that hadith does not take one out of the Hanafi school; rather, it is considered the Imam's own madhhab because he would have followed strong evidence. The author clarifies that this rule applies only to those capable of understanding texts and distinguishing clear from abrogated. It is a refinement of the earlier rule on disciples' statements, reinforcing that the Hanafi madhhab is broader than any single ruling—it accommodates following stronger evidence when available.

Key Terms
إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي (idhā ṣaḥḥa al-ḥadīthu fahūwa madhhabī): A principle stating that if a hadith is authentically attributed to the Prophet, it represents Imam Abu Hanifa's true madhhab, even if it contradicts what is commonly known as the Hanafi position.

D) Only the disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي' allows qualified Hanafi scholars to issue fatwas based on sahih hadith even when contrary to the established ruling, remaining within the madhhab.

The Principle 'If the Hadith is Authentic, It Is My Madhhab' — Conditions and Limitations
Rasm al-mufti rule with conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of acting upon an authentic hadith contrary to the madhhab, identify the conditions that restrict its application, and apply the hierarchy of madhhab authority in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
An authentic hadith can override the madhhab only for the qualified scholar, and never against school consensus.

One of the most frequently cited — and misunderstood — principles in Hanafi ifta is the statement attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab.' This lesson unpacks Ibn Abidin’s treatment of this rule in his Rasm al-Mufti. You will see that the rule is not a blank cheque for abandoning the madhhab. Rather, it comes with strict conditions: the scholar must be qualified to evaluate abrogation, the hadith must align with some existing opinion within the school, and it cannot override the consensus of the early imams. We will also examine an exceptional case where necessity justifies deviation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle and its source
Read the opening of the passage and identify the statement 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab.' Note that Ibn Abidin cites this from al-Bayri, who traces it to Abu Hanifa. Understand that this is a foundational maxim for Hanafi fatwa.
2
Analyze the first condition: qualification of the scholar
Ibn Abidin immediately qualifies: this rule is for 'one who is qualified to examine texts and know the abrogating from the abrogated.' Reflect on why knowledge of naskh is critical: a hadith that seems authentic may be abrogated. Only a scholar with ijtihad-level skills can apply this principle.
The principle does not apply to lay muqallids.
3
Examine the second condition: consistency with the madhhab
Ibn Abidin restricts the rule further: it applies only when the hadith agrees with a statement already present in the madhhab. He argues that the early imams' consensus represents stronger ijtihad; their silence on a hadith likely means they had evidence against it.
Thus, the rule cannot be used to introduce a ruling entirely outside the school's framework.
4
Understand the exception: necessity (darurah)
The passage notes that in cases of necessity, such as hiring for teaching Quran to prevent religious loss, the mufti may deviate from agreed rulings. This is a limited concession to preserve the religion, not a general license.
This exception is found in al-Hawi al-Qudsi and other sources.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
The passage cites Qadi Khan's statement that the mufti must follow the apparent narrations without disagreement and not override them with personal opinion. This sets the hierarchy: zahir al-riwayah > personal ijtihad, unless weakness is proven or necessity arises.
The mufta bihi is the madhhab's agreed position; deviation requires strong justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العمل بالحديث إذا صح]
ونظير هذا ما نقله العلامة البيري في أول شرحه على "الأشباه" عن شرح الهداية" لابن الشحنة الكبير، والد شارح "الوهبانية" وشيخ ابن الهمام، ونصه: "إذا صح الحديث وكان على خلاف المذهب، عمل بالحديث، ويكون ذلك مذهبه ولا يخرج مقلده عن كونه حنفيا بالعمل به، فقد صح عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال: "إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي"، وقد حكى ذلك الإمام ابن عبد البر عن أبي حنيفة وغيره من الأئمة". ونقله أيضا الإمام الشعراني عن الأئمة الأربعة.
:قلت ولا يخفى أن ذلك لمن كان أهلا للنظر في النصوص، ومعرفة محكمها من منسوخها. فإذا نظر أهل المذهب في الدليل، وعملوا به نسبته إلى المذهب؛ لكونه صادرا بإذن صاحب المذهب؛ إذ لا شك صح أنه لو علم بضعف دليله رجع عنه، واتبع الدليل الأقوى. ولذا رد المحقق ابن الهمام على المشايخ حيث أفتوا بقول الإمامين، بأنه لا يعدل عن قول الإمام إلا لضعف دليله.
وأقول أيضا ينبغي تقييد ذلك بما إذا وافق قولا في المذهب؛ إذ لم يأذنوا في الاجتهاد فيما خرج عن المذهب بالكلية مما اتفق عليه أئمتنا؛ لأن اجتهادهم أقوى من اجتهاده فالظاهر أنهم رأوا دليلا أرجح مما رآه حتى لم يعملوا به، ولهذا قال العلامة قاسم في حق شيخه خاتمة المحققين الكمال بن الهمام: لا يعمل بأبحاث شيخنا التي تخالف المذهب"، وقال في تصحيحه على القدوري: قال الإمام العلامة الحسن بن منصور بن محمود الأوزجندي المعروف بـ"قاضي خان" في كتاب الفتاوى: "رسم المفتي في زماننا من أصحابنا إذا استفتي عن مسألة إن كانت مروية عن أصحابنا في الروايات الظاهرة بلا خلاف بينهم، فإنه يميل إليهم ويفتي بقولهم، ولا يخالفهم برأيه وإن كان مجتهدا متقنا؛ لأن الظاهر أن يكون الحق مع أصحابنا ولا يعدوهم واجتهاده لا يبلغ اجتهادهم، ولا ينظر إلى قول من خالفهم، ولا تقبل حجته أيضا؛ لأنهم عرفوا الأدلة، وميزوا بين ما صح وثبت وبين ضده إلخ. ثم نقل نحوه عن شرح برهان الأئمة على أدب القضاء" للخصاف.
:قلت لكن ربما عدلوا عما اتفق عليه أئمتنا لضرورة ونحوها، كما مر في مسألة الاستئجار على تعليم القرآن ونحوه من الطاعات التي في ترك الاستئجار عليها ضياع الدين كما قررناه سابقا، فحينئذ يجوز الإفتاء بخلاف قولهم، كما نذكره قريبا عن "الحاوي القدسي"، وسيأتي بسطه أيضا آخر الشرح عند الكلام على العرف.
[The ruling: acting upon the hadith if authentic.]
Analogous to this is what Allamah al-Bayri narrated at the beginning of his commentary on al-Ashbah from the commentary of al-Hidayah by Ibn al-Shahnah al-Kabir, father of the commentator of al-Wahbaniyyah and teacher of Ibn al-Humam: "If the hadith is authentic and contrary to the madhhab, act upon the hadith, and that becomes his madhhab, and his follower does not cease to be a Hanafi by acting on it. It is authentically reported from Abu Hanifa that he said: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab.' This has been reported by Ibn Abd al-Barr from Abu Hanifa and others." Also narrated by al-Sha'rani from the four imams.
I say: It is obvious that this is for one who is qualified to examine texts and know the abrogating from the abrogated. When the people of the madhhab examine the evidence and act upon it, it is attributed to the madhhab because it comes by permission of the founder; there is no doubt that if he knew the weakness of his evidence, he would retract and follow the stronger evidence. Hence, the verifier Ibn al-Humam criticized the scholars who gave fatwa according to the statement of the two companions, saying that one should not deviate from the Imam's statement except due to weakness of his evidence.
I also say: This should be restricted to when it agrees with a statement in the madhhab, because they did not permit ijtihad in matters that completely exit the madhhab and are agreed upon by our imams; for their ijtihad is stronger than his, and evidently they saw a stronger evidence and therefore did not act upon it. Thus Allamah Qasim said about his teacher, the seal of the verifiers Kamal Ibn al-Humam: "One should not act upon the investigations of our teacher that contradict the madhhab." And in his correction on al-Quduri, he said: Imam al-Hasan ibn Mansur al-Uzjandi, known as Qadi Khan, said in the book al-Fatawa: "The practice of the mufti in our time among our companions: if he is asked about a matter that is narrated from our companions in the apparent narrations without disagreement, he leans toward them and gives fatwa according to their statement, and does not contradict them with his own opinion even if he is a diligent verifier, because it is apparent that the truth is with our companions and does go beyond them; his ijtihad does not reach their ijtihad. He does not look at the statement of those who contradict them, nor is his proof accepted, because they know the evidence and distinguish between what is authentic and established and the opposite." Then he narrated similar from the commentary of Burhan al-A'imma on Adab al-Qada of al-Khassaf.
I say: However, they might deviate from what our imams agreed upon due to necessity and the like, as mentioned in the issue of hiring for teaching the Quran and similar acts of obedience where abandoning hiring would cause the loss of the religion, as we previously established. Thus it is permissible to give fatwa contrary to their statement, as we will mention soon from al-Hawi al-Qudsi, and its details will come later in the commentary when discussing custom.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a central rule for Hanafi ifta: acting upon an authentic hadith even if it contradicts the madhhab. It opens with a heading: 'Acting upon the hadith if authentic.' The author then cites al-Bayri narrating the famous statement: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab,' which has been attributed to Abu Hanifa and other imams. However, Ibn Abidin immediately qualifies this: it applies only to someone qualified to evaluate abrogation and evidence. If such a person finds evidence stronger, the ruling becomes part of the madhhab because the imam would agree if he knew the weakness of his own evidence. Then the author criticizes Ibn al-Humam's opposition to muftis who follow Abu Yusuf and Muhammad over Abu Hanifa, explaining that deviation from the imam is only allowed when his evidence is weak. Next, he restricts the rule further: it only applies when the hadith aligns with some opinion already within the madhhab; one cannot invent a ruling completely outside the school's consensus, because the early imams' ijtihad is stronger. He supports this with Qadi Khan's statement that the mufti must follow the established narrated opinions and not override them with personal reasoning. Finally, he notes an exception: for necessities like hiring for Quran teaching, where abandoning the agreed ruling preserves the religion, deviation is permitted. This shows the principle's balance between following authentic evidence and maintaining madhhab coherence.

Key Terms
العمل بالحديث إذا صح (al-'amal bi'l-hadith idha sahha): The principle that an authentic hadith, even if contrary to the madhhab, can be acted upon and becomes the madhhab for the qualified scholar.
مذهب (madhhab): The school of Abu Hanifa; includes both direct statements and rulings derived by disciples using his principles and approved by school authorities.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning; here, the ability to weigh evidence and determine rulings.
محكم ومنسوخ (muhkam wa mansukh): Abrogating and abrogated texts; necessary knowledge for evaluating if a hadith is applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Abidin, what is a necessary condition for the principle 'if the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' to apply?
A) The hadith must be from Sahih al-Bukhari.
B) The person must be qualified to examine texts and understand abrogation.
C) The hadith must be followed by all four schools.
D) The mufti must have memorized the entire Quran.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'if authentic, it is my madhhab' is a real but restricted rule in Hanafi ifta: it applies only to qualified scholars, must align with the madhhab, and never overrides the school's consensus except for necessity.

The Mufti's Duty: Following the Madhhab and the Necessity Exception
Rasm al-mufti rule with necessity exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule for adherence to the madhhab and apply the necessity exception to a case of potential religious neglect.

Core Idea
The mufti is bound by the madhhab; deviation requires necessity and a basis within the school.

This passage navigates a critical tension for the mufti: loyalty to the established madhhab versus the need to address unique circumstances. The great Hanafi jurist Qadi Khan lays down the default rule—follow the transmitted positions without personal ijtihad—but the commentator adds a crucial caveat: necessity can justify a limited departure. Mastering this balance is essential for responsible fatwa-giving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Rule
Find the core principle from Qadi Khan: when a matter is narrated in the apparent narrations (zahir al-riwaya) without disagreement, the mufti must lean towards that position and give fatwa accordingly, even if he is a capable mujtahid.
The rule applies only to matters where the madhhab is settled.
2
Understand the Restriction
Notice the author's refinement: the default rule only holds when the fatwa corresponds to a statement within the madhhab. The scholars never permitted ijtihad that entirely exits the madhhab on issues the founding imams agreed upon.
This restriction prevents arbitrary departure.
3
Recognize the Exception
The author introduces an exception: necessity (darurah) or similar grounds can justify deviation from what the imams agreed upon. The example given is hiring for teaching the Qur'an—if prohibited, religion may suffer.
The exception must still align with the school's principles and is not a carte blanche for ijtihad.
4
Apply to the Example
Consider the case of hiring for teaching the Qur'an. Normally, hiring for acts of worship may be disallowed. But if neglecting this leads to the ruin of religious knowledge, the necessity exception permits a fatwa allowing it, even if it contradicts the stronger view.
This shows the mufti's role in balancing textual rulings with public interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العمل بالحديث إذا صح ابن الهمام على المشايخ حيث أفتوا بقول الإمامين، بأنه لا يعدل عن قول الإمام إلا لضعف دليله. وأقول أيضا ينبغي تقييد ذلك بما إذا وافق قولا في المذهب؛ إذ لم يأذنوا في الاجتهاد فيما خرج عن المذهب بالكلية مما اتفق عليه أئمتنا؛ لأن اجتهادهم أقوى من اجتهاده فالظاهر أنهم رأوا دليلا أرجح مما رآه حتى لم يعملوا به، ولهذا قال العلامة قاسم في حق شيخه خاتمة المحققين الكمال بن الهمام: لا يعمل بأبحاث شيخنا التي تخالف المذهب"، وقال في تصحيحه على القدوري: قال الإمام العلامة الحسن بن منصور بن محمود الأوزجندي المعروف بـ"قاضي خان" في كتاب الفتاوى: "رسم المفتي في زماننا من أصحابنا إذا استفتي عن مسألة إن كانت مروية عن أصحابنا في الروايات الظاهرة بلا خلاف بينهم، فإنه يميل إليهم ويفتي بقولهم، ولا يخالفهم برأيه وإن كان مجتهدا متقنا؛ لأن الظاهر أن يكون الحق مع أصحابنا ولا يعدوهم واجتهاده لا يبلغ اجتهادهم، ولا ينظر إلى قول من خالفهم، ولا تقبل حجته أيضا؛ لأنهم عرفوا الأدلة، وميزوا بين ما صح وثبت وبين ضده إلخ. ثم نقل نحوه عن شرح برهان الأئمة على أدب القضاء" للخصاف. :قلت لكن ربما عدلوا عما اتفق عليه أئمتنا لضرورة ونحوها، كما مر في مسألة الاستئجار على تعليم القرآن ونحوه من الطاعات التي في ترك الاستئجار عليها ضياع الدين كما قررناه سابقا، فحينئذ يجوز الإفتاء بخلاف قولهم، كما نذكره قريبا عن "الحاوي القدسي"، وسيأتي بسطه أيضا آخر الشرح عند الكلام على العرف.
Adhering to the hadith if authentic. Ibn al-Humam criticized the scholars who gave fatwa according to the statement of the two Imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), that one should not deviate from the Imam's statement except due to weakness of his evidence. I also say: it is appropriate to restrict that to when it agrees with a statement in the madhhab, since they did not permit ijtihad in matters that exit the madhhab entirely, which our Imams have agreed upon, because their ijtihad is stronger than his ijtihad. It appears that they saw evidence more preponderant than what he saw, to the point that they did not act upon it. For this reason, Allamah Qasim said regarding his teacher, the seal of the investigators Kamal ibn al-Humam: 'One should not act upon our teacher's investigations that contradict the madhhab.' And in his correction of al-Quduri, he said: Imam, Allamah al-Hasan ibn Mansur ibn Mahmud al-Uzjani known as Qadi Khan said in the book of Fatawa: 'The rule of the mufti in our time from our companions: when he is asked about a matter, if it is narrated from our companions in the apparent narrations without disagreement among them, then he leans towards them and gives fatwa according to their statement, and he does not oppose them with his opinion even if he is a proficient mujtahid, because it is apparent that the truth is with our companions and he does not go beyond them, and his ijtihad does not reach their ijtihad. He should not look at the statement of those who oppose them, nor accept their evidence, because they know the evidences and distinguish between what is authentic and established and its opposite, etc.' Then he narrated something similar from Sharh Burhan al-A'imma 'ala Adab al-Qada' by al-Khassaf. I say: but perhaps they deviate from what our Imams agreed upon due to necessity and the like, as mentioned in the issue of hiring for teaching the Qur'an and similar acts of obedience, in which the abandonment of hiring leads to the ruin of the religion, as we have established previously. In that case, it is permissible to give fatwa contrary to their statement, as we will mention soon from al-Hawi al-Qudsi, and its elaboration will also come at the end of the commentary when discussing custom.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule concerning the mufti's obligation to adhere to the established madhhab. The author begins by referencing a statement of Ibn al-Humam criticizing scholars who give fatwa according to the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) while deviating from Abu Hanifa's opinion. Ibn al-Humam apparently held that one should not deviate from the Imam's statement unless his evidence is weak. The author then refines this: the rule applies only when the fatwa corresponds to a position within the madhhab; the scholars never permitted ijtihad that entirely exits the madhhab on issues agreed upon by the founding imams, because their ijtihad is stronger. The author then quotes Qadi Khan's famous rule: a mufti in our time, when asked about a matter that is narrated in the apparent narrations without disagreement, should incline to those narrations and give fatwa accordingly, not opposing them with his own opinion even if he is a mujtahid, because the truth lies with the imams and his ijtihad does not reach theirs. However, the author adds an important exception: necessity or similar grounds may justify deviation from the agreed-upon madhhab, citing the example of hiring for teaching the Qur'an—if hiring is prohibited, the religion might be neglected due to lack of teachers. In such cases, giving fatwa contrary to the madhhab is permissible. This shows that while the mufti is bound by the madhhab, he is not absolutely locked into it; extraordinary circumstances can warrant departure, provided the new ruling still aligns with the school's underlying principles.

Key Terms
رسم المفتي (rasm al-mufti): The rule or guideline that a mufti must follow when issuing fatwas, especially regarding which opinions to prefer from the madhhab.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that may justify a deviation from the established ruling, provided the alternative is still within the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rasm al-mufti rule quoted from Qadi Khan, what should a mufti do when a matter is narrated in the apparent narrations without disagreement?
A) He should exercise his own ijtihad and issue a fatwa based on his reasoning.
B) He should lean towards the narrated statement and give fatwa accordingly, not opposing it with his own opinion.
C) He may choose any opinion he finds convincing, including those outside the madhhab.
D) He must refer the question to a higher authority since he cannot decide.

Key Takeaway
The mufti is bound by the madhhab but may deviate for genuine necessity, provided the alternative statement exists within the school.

Attribution of Takhrijat: The Qa'idah of Ibn Abidin
Explains the qa'idah and its dabt for attributing takhrijat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qa'idah regarding takhrijat and apply the correct attribution phrase (muqtada al-madhhab) when a ruling is derived from Imam Abu Hanifa's principles.

Core Idea
Takhrijat based on Imam's qawaid are part of his madhhab, but must be attributed as 'muqtada al-madhhab', not as his direct statement.

This lesson introduces a key rule from Ibn Abidin's Rasm al-Mufti: rulings derived by later scholars (takhrijat) from Imam Abu Hanifa's legal principles are considered part of his madhhab, but they must be attributed carefully. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis and judges to maintain scholarly integrity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qa'idah
Read the opening sentence: 'ما بناه المشايخ على قواعد الإمام لا يخرج عن مذهبه'. What is the main claim?
2
Understand the Dabt (Restriction)
The author adds: 'لا يقال فيه: قال أبو حنيفة، بل يقال: مقتضى مذهبه'. Why is this restriction important?
3
Apply to Examples
Consider two scenarios: a mufti issuing a fatwa on a trade contract using analogy on Imam's statements, and a judge ruling on a divorce case using a takhrij. What should each say?
4
Distinguish Explicit vs. Derived
How does the passage differentiate between explicit narrations from Imam and derived rulings? When can we say 'Qala Abu Hanifa'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تخريجات المشايخ على قواعد الإمام
والحاصل أن ما خالف فيها الأصحاب إمامهم الأعظم، لا يخرج عن مذهبه إذا رجحه المشايخ المعتبرون، وكذا ما بناه المشايخ على العرف الحادث لتغير الزمان أو للضرورة، ونحو ذلك لا يخرج عن مذهبه أيضاً؛ لأن ما رجحوه لترجح دليله عندهم مأذون به من جهة الإمام، وكذا ما بنوه على تغير الزمان والضرورة، باعتبار أنه لو كان حيا لقال بما قالوه؛
لأن ما قالوه إنما هو مبني على قواعده أيضا، فهو مقتضى مذهبه. لكن ينبغي أن لا يقال: "قال أبو حنيفة كذا"، إلا فيما روي عنه صريحا، وإنما يقال فيه: "مقتضى مذهب أبي حنيفة كذا"، كما قلنا. ومثله تخريجات المشايخ بعض الأحكام من قواعده، أو بالقياس على قوله، ومنه قولهم: "وعلى قياس قوله بكذا يكون كذا"، فهذا كله لا يقال فيه: "قال أبو حنيفة"، نعم! يصح أن يسمى مذهبه بمعنى أنه قول أهل مذهبه، أو مقتضى مذهبه.
وعن هذا لمّا قال صاحب "الدرر والغرر" في كتاب القضاء: "إذا قضى القاضي في مجتهد فيه بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ" - قال: "أي أصل المذهب كالحنفي إذا حكم على مذهب الشافعي أو نحوه، أو بالعكس، وأما إذا حكم الحنفي بمذهب أبي يوسف، أو محمد، أو نحوهما من أصحاب الإمام، فليس حكما بخلاف رأيه". والظاهر أن نسبة المسائل المخرّجة إلى مذهبه أقرب من نسبة المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف أو محمد إليه؛ لأن المخرجة مبنية على قواعده وأصوله، وأما المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف ونحوه من أصحاب الإمام، فكثير منها مبني على قواعد لهم خالفوا فيها قواعد الإمام؛ لأنهم لم يلتزموا قواعده كلها، كما يعرفه من له معرفة بكتب الأصول. نعم! قد يقال: إذا كانت أقوالهم روايات عنه - على ما مر – تكون تلك القواعد له أيضا لابتناء تلك الأقوال عليها. وعلى هذا أيضا تكون نسبة التخريجات إلى مذهبه أقرب ؛ لابتنائها على قواعده التي رجحها وبنى أقواله عليها. فإذا قضى القاضي بما صح منها، نفذ قضاؤه، كما ينفذ بما صح من أقوال الأصحاب.
فهذا ما ظهر لي تقريره في هذا الباب من فتح الملك الوهاب، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب، وإليه المرجع والمآب.
وحيث لم يوجد له اختيار فقول يعقوبَ هو المختارُ محمد فقوله الحسنُ ثم زفر وابن زياد الحسن
ثم
وقيل بالتخيير في فتواه إن خالف الإمام صاحباه وقيل من دليله أقوى رُجح وذا لمفت ذي اجتهاد الأصح
Derivations of the scholars on the rules of the Imam. The summary is that what the companions differed in from their greatest Imam does not depart from his madhhab if the reliable scholars have given it preponderance, and likewise what the scholars built upon the newly emerged custom due to change of time or necessity, and similar things, also do not depart from his madhhab; because what they preponderated due to the preponderance of its proof according to them is authorized by the Imam, and likewise what they built upon change of time and necessity, with consideration that if he were alive he would have said what they said; because what they said is also built upon his rules, so it is the requirement of his madhhab. But it should not be said: "Abu Hanifa said such-and-such", except in what is explicitly narrated from him; rather it should be said: "The requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such-and-such", as we said. And similar to that are the derivations of some rulings from his rules, or by analogy on his statement, and from that is their saying: "And on the analogy of his statement such-and-such, it is such-and-such". All this is not said: "Abu Hanifa said". Yes! It is valid to call it his madhhab in the sense that it is the statement of the people of his madhhab, or the requirement of his madhhab. Regarding this, when the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar said in the book of Judgments: "If a judge rules in a matter of ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, it is not executed" — he said: "i.e., the original madhhab, like a Hanafi if he judges according to the Shafi'i madhhab or similar, or vice versa. But if a Hanafi judges according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad, or similar among the companions of the Imam, it is not a ruling contrary to his opinion." And it is apparent that the attribution of derived issues to his madhhab is closer than the attribution of issues stated by Abu Yusuf or Muhammad to him; because the derived issues are based on his rules and principles, whereas many issues stated by Abu Yusuf and similar companions of the Imam are based on their own rules in which they differed from the rules of the Imam; because they did not commit to all his rules, as is known by one who has knowledge of books of usul. Yes! It may be said: If their statements are narrations from him — as was mentioned — then those rules are also his due to the derivation of those statements upon them. And on this basis, the attribution of derivations to his madhhab is also closer, due to their being based on his rules which he preponderated and built his statements upon. So if a judge judges according to what is sound among them, his judgment is executed, just as it is executed according to sound statements of the companions. So this is what appeared to me as its determination in this chapter from Fath al-Malik al-Wahhab, and Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the abode. And where his choice is not found, the statement of Ya'qub is the chosen; then the statement of Muhammad; then al-Hasan; then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad al-Hasan; then it is said there is option in his fatwa if the two companions opposed the Imam; and it is said: whose proof is stronger is preponderated, and this is most correct for a mufti who has ijtihad.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Abidin's commentary on Rasm al-Mufti clarifies an important rule in Hanafi ifta: the status of rulings derived by later scholars (takhrijat) based on Imam Abu Hanifa's legal principles (qawaid). The author affirms that such derivations are part of the Hanafi madhhab, as they are built on the Imam's own rules. However, one must be precise in attribution: it is incorrect to say 'Abu Hanifa said this' unless there is explicit narration; rather, one should say 'the requirement of Abu Hanifa's madhhab is this.' The same applies to rulings based on custom change or necessity. The passage then discusses a quote from al-Durar wa al-Ghurar about a judge ruling contrary to his madhhab: if a Hanafi judge rules according to Abu Yusuf or Muhammad, it is not a violation of his madhhab because these are within the school. Interestingly, the author argues that derived issues are even more closely tied to the Imam's madhhab than the direct statements of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad, because the latter sometimes departed from the Imam's principles. The text concludes with a poetic enumeration of the hierarchy of preference among the major Hanafi imams: Abu Yusuf's opinion is chosen, then Muhammad's, then al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's, then Zufar's and others. If the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) both oppose the Imam, there is a difference of opinion: either the mufti has a choice or should follow the stronger proof. The preferred view for a mufti capable of ijtihad is to follow the stronger evidence.

Key Terms
تخريجات المشايخ (takhrijat al-mashayikh): Rulings derived by later Hanafi scholars from Imam Abu Hanifa's legal principles (qawaid) and analogies, not explicitly stated by the Imam himself.
قواعد الإمام (qawaid al-imam): The foundational legal principles (usul) and maxims upon which Imam Abu Hanifa built his rulings.
مقتضى المذهب (muqtada al-madhhab): The logical implication or requirement of the madhhab's principles, used to attribute derived rulings to the Imam without claiming direct statement.
الأصحاب (al-ashab): The major companions of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Abidin, when can a mufti say 'Qala Abu Hanifa'?
A) Whenever the ruling is derived from the Imam's qawaid.
B) Only when the ruling is explicitly narrated from the Imam.
C) Only when the ruling is from the companions of the Imam.
D) Whenever the ruling is consistent with the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Takeaway
Takhrijat based on Imam Abu Hanifa's principles are part of his madhhab but must be attributed as 'muqtada al-madhhab', not as his direct statement.

Application in Qada and Mufti Hierarchy
Applies the qa'idah to qada and gives hierarchy for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of takhrijat to judicial rulings and identify the hierarchy of Hanafi imams for fatwa.

Core Idea
A Hanafi judge may enforce a takhrij ruling from later scholars; the hierarchy of imams for fatwa is: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Hasan, Zufar.

This lesson continues from the previous one, applying the qa'idah to the context of judicial rulings (qada) and presenting the established hierarchy among the major Hanafi imams for issuing fatwas. The passage clarifies that judging by a takhrij is not a violation of the madhhab, and outlines the preferred order of reliance.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Qada Quote
Read the quote from al-Durar wa al-Ghurar: 'إذا قضى القاضي في مجتهد فيه بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ'. How does Ibn Abidin interpret 'madhhab' here?
2
Compare Takhrij and Ashab
Ibn Abidin argues that takhrijat are closer to the Imam's madhhab than some opinions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Why?
3
Recall the Hierarchy

4
Apply to Fatwa
If a mufti encounters a case with no explicit narration from Imam, and the ashab differ, what should he do?

وعن هذا لمّا قال صاحب "الدرر والغرر" في كتاب القضاء: "إذا قضى القاضي في مجتهد فيه بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ" - قال: "أي أصل المذهب كالحنفي إذا حكم على مذهب الشافعي أو نحوه، أو بالعكس، وأما إذا حكم الحنفي بمذهب أبي يوسف، أو محمد، أو نحوهما من أصحاب الإمام، فليس حكما بخلاف رأيه". والظاهر أن نسبة المسائل المخرّجة إلى مذهبه أقرب من نسبة المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف أو محمد إليه؛ لأن المخرجة مبنية على قواعده وأصوله، وأما المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف ونحوه من أصحاب الإمام، فكثير منها مبني على قواعد لهم خالفوا فيها قواعد الإمام؛ لأنهم لم يلتزموا قواعده كلها، كما يعرفه من له معرفة بكتب الأصول. نعم! قد يقال: إذا كانت أقوالهم روايات عنه - على ما مر – تكون تلك القواعد له أيضا لابتناء تلك الأقوال عليها. وعلى هذا أيضا تكون نسبة التخريجات إلى مذهبه أقرب ؛ لابتنائها على قواعده التي رجحها وبنى أقواله عليها. فإذا قضى القاضي بما صح منها، نفذ قضاؤه، كما ينفذ بما صح من أقوال الأصحاب.
فهذا ما ظهر لي تقريره في هذا الباب من فتح الملك الوهاب، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب، وإليه المرجع والمآب.
وحيث لم يوجد له اختيار فقول يعقوبَ هو المختارُ محمد فقوله الحسنُ ثم زفر وابن زياد الحسن
ثم
وقيل بالتخيير في فتواه إن خالف الإمام صاحباه وقيل من دليله أقوى رُجح وذا لمفت ذي اجتهاد الأصح
Derivations of the scholars on the rules of the Imam. The summary is that what the companions differed in from their greatest Imam does not depart from his madhhab if the reliable scholars have given it preponderance, and likewise what the scholars built upon the newly emerged custom due to change of time or necessity, and similar things, also do not depart from his madhhab; because what they preponderated due to the preponderance of its proof according to them is authorized by the Imam, and likewise what they built upon change of time and necessity, with consideration that if he were alive he would have said what they said; because what they said is also built upon his rules, so it is the requirement of his madhhab. But it should not be said: "Abu Hanifa said such-and-such", except in what is explicitly narrated from him; rather it should be said: "The requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such-and-such", as we said. And similar to that are the derivations of some rulings from his rules, or by analogy on his statement, and from that is their saying: "And on the analogy of his statement such-and-such, it is such-and-such". All this is not said: "Abu Hanifa said". Yes! It is valid to call it his madhhab in the sense that it is the statement of the people of his madhhab, or the requirement of his madhhab. Regarding this, when the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar said in the book of Judgments: "If a judge rules in a matter of ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, it is not executed" — he said: "i.e., the original madhhab, like a Hanafi if he judges according to the Shafi'i madhhab or similar, or vice versa. But if a Hanafi judges according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad, or similar among the companions of the Imam, it is not a ruling contrary to his opinion." And it is apparent that the attribution of derived issues to his madhhab is closer than the attribution of issues stated by Abu Yusuf or Muhammad to him; because the derived issues are based on his rules and principles, whereas many issues stated by Abu Yusuf and similar companions of the Imam are based on their own rules in which they differed from the rules of the Imam; because they did not commit to all his rules, as is known by one who has knowledge of books of usul. Yes! It may be said: If their statements are narrations from him — as was mentioned — then those rules are also his due to the derivation of those statements upon them. And on this basis, the attribution of derivations to his madhhab is also closer, due to their being based on his rules which he preponderated and built his statements upon. So if a judge judges according to what is sound among them, his judgment is executed, just as it is executed according to sound statements of the companions. So this is what appeared to me as its determination in this chapter from Fath al-Malik al-Wahhab, and Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the abode. And where his choice is not found, the statement of Ya'qub is the chosen; then the statement of Muhammad; then al-Hasan; then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad al-Hasan; then it is said there is option in his fatwa if the two companions opposed the Imam; and it is said: whose proof is stronger is preponderated, and this is most correct for a mufti who has ijtihad.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Abidin's commentary on Rasm al-Mufti clarifies an important rule in Hanafi ifta: the status of rulings derived by later scholars (takhrijat) based on Imam Abu Hanifa's legal principles (qawaid). The author affirms that such derivations are part of the Hanafi madhhab, as they are built on the Imam's own rules. However, one must be precise in attribution: it is incorrect to say 'Abu Hanifa said this' unless there is explicit narration; rather, one should say 'the requirement of Abu Hanifa's madhhab is this.' The same applies to rulings based on custom change or necessity. The passage then discusses a quote from al-Durar wa al-Ghurar about a judge ruling contrary to his madhhab: if a Hanafi judge rules according to Abu Yusuf or Muhammad, it is not a violation of his madhhab because these are within the school. Interestingly, the author argues that derived issues are even more closely tied to the Imam's madhhab than the direct statements of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad, because the latter sometimes departed from the Imam's principles. The text concludes with a poetic enumeration of the hierarchy of preference among the major Hanafi imams: Abu Yusuf's opinion is chosen, then Muhammad's, then al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's, then Zufar's and others. If the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) both oppose the Imam, there is a difference of opinion: either the mufti has a choice or should follow the stronger proof. The preferred view for a mufti capable of ijtihad is to follow the stronger evidence.

Key Terms
تخريجات المشايخ (takhrijat al-mashayikh): Rulings derived by later Hanafi scholars from Imam Abu Hanifa's legal principles (qawaid) and analogies, not explicitly stated by the Imam himself.
قواعد الإمام (qawaid al-imam): The foundational legal principles (usul) and maxims upon which Imam Abu Hanifa built his rulings.
مقتضى المذهب (muqtada al-madhhab): The logical implication or requirement of the madhhab's principles, used to attribute derived rulings to the Imam without claiming direct statement.
الأصحاب (al-ashab): The major companions of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct ruling when a Hanafi judge rules according to a takhrij based on Imam Abu Hanifa's principles?
A) The judgment is void because it is not from an explicit Imam statement.
B) The judgment is valid and enforceable.
C) The judgment is valid only if the takhrij was issued by a mujtahid.
D) The judgment is void because takhrijat are not part of the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi judge can enforce takhrij rulings and companion opinions within the school; the hierarchy for fatwa is Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Hasan, Zufar; when the two companions oppose the Imam, a mufti with ijtihad follows the stronger proof.
